    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_113304239" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/113304239" />
    <a title="Guy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2899659-guy"><img alt="Guy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1257131915p2/2899659.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/113304239?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Guy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Guy" href="/user/show/2899659-guy">Guy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer113304239" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2075879362208568936"><br/><br/><br/>When I bought this book, second hand but 'new,' I ignored the little alarms that warned me to keep my money in my pocket. I had spent too much time looking for my usual dreck in my local used bookstore, and had made myself late — books before life! As I'm in the process of leaving the store I see atop an 'in-box' near the cash register <i>Beauty and Sadness</i>. I decided that the author being Japanese out-weighed my caution against him being a Nobel prize winner. I allowed my visual aesthetic to tumbl</span>
  <span id="freeText2075879362208568936" style="display:none"><br /><br /><br />When I bought this book, second hand but 'new,' I ignored the little alarms that warned me to keep my money in my pocket. I had spent too much time looking for my usual dreck in my local used bookstore, and had made myself late — books before life! As I'm in the process of leaving the store I see atop an 'in-box' near the cash register <i>Beauty and Sadness</i>. I decided that the author being Japanese out-weighed my caution against him being a Nobel prize winner. I allowed my visual aesthetic to tumble me into an infatuation with the Japanese print without reading the publication details. And so it came to pass that I impulsively bought a Japanese version of Henry James because I was in a hurry.<br /><br />Henry James! I had rather throw sand in my eyes than read HJ! But, there I was. I spent my time reading this book wondering at whether or not it was the translator or the author who had effected the dull thud of short sentences filled with ominous meaning spoken by automatons repetitively about slowly rotating chairs, or obis, or paintings over and over again, repetitively.<br /><br />The different characters all spoke in the same manner, with the same cadence, and the same heavy handed overtures to misplaced meaningfulness in a meaningless life. There were several times when I had to re-read the dialogue in order to keep straight who was speaking because the sentences all sounded as if they were spoken by the same person.<br /><br />And the passions expressed were done in such dead voices that the finale was almost funny in its obviousness. Now that I'm older, it strikes me that James' characters tend to sound emotionally like overly melodramatic teenagers, housed, supposedly, in the bodies of adults with the adults' life experiences but twisted by their youthful fixation on nirvanic virginal sex, unrequited puppy love, and a cloyingly repugnant narcissistic infantilism. And this is exactly what Yasunari Kawabata and/or his translator gave us with <i>Beauty and Sadness</i>.<br /><br />Why did I finish reading it, then? Well, in short, because I foolishly fell back into my own version of infantilism, to a time when I took pride in my having finished reading every book I started, regardless whether or not I enjoyed it. Michener's <i>The Source</i> humbled me in that regard, and with his writing sparked my nascent understanding that reading bad writing is a narcissistic waste of life. Life is short; read the good books first! (Okay, okay, what is a good book is hard objectively to define!)<br /><br />And so why did I finish reading <i>Beauty and Sadness</i>? Because I belong to some weird web-based book club, and I wanted to put another book into my 'read' file; and because I wanted to write a review of it that I could put up into the ether-sphere. Oh! And because the book is very short, with relatively large font, and is festooned with lots of white space. And when I write this review I get to stuff it on other web sites, at least one which offers a chance at winning some money for books.<br /><br />If you like Henry James, you'll probably like this. If you find youthful melodrama played out by so-called adults with emotionless sensitivity trite and trying, give this book a pass.<br /><br />Moral of the story? When buying books, do not ignore the small inner intuitive warnings lest your book buy's haste has bought you waste.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2075879362208568936" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2478506765" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2478506765" />
    <a title="Ilana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59371159-ilana"><img alt="Ilana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563253235p2/59371159.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2478506765?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ilana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ilana" href="/user/show/59371159-ilana">Ilana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/25784171-beauty-and-sadness">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=nobel-prize">nobel-prize</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=japan">japan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=guardian-1000">guardian-1000</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59371159-ilana?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2478506765" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13126879650570671003">Some 25 years after Toshio Oki's affair with then fifteen-year-old Otoko Ueno, Toshio, feeling nostalgic, decides to visit a now mature Otoko to listen to the ringing of the New Year Eve's bells in her town of Kyoto. Toshio is a successful author who made a name for himself by writing a novel based on the romance he had as a 30-year-old man with the young and passionate girl. An affair he had while his own wife was pregnant and expecting their first child. Otoko became pregnant as well, but lost</span>
  <span id="freeText13126879650570671003" style="display:none">Some 25 years after Toshio Oki's affair with then fifteen-year-old Otoko Ueno, Toshio, feeling nostalgic, decides to visit a now mature Otoko to listen to the ringing of the New Year Eve's bells in her town of Kyoto. Toshio is a successful author who made a name for himself by writing a novel based on the romance he had as a 30-year-old man with the young and passionate girl. An affair he had while his own wife was pregnant and expecting their first child. Otoko became pregnant as well, but lost the baby shortly after giving birth to a little girl. Otoko is now a reclusive artist living with her protégée and young lover, Keiko, a young girl gifted with talent as an artist in her own right, and a sharp taste for vengeance. When he meets the two women together for the New Year Eve event, Keiko decides she will avenge her mistress and begins a manipulative game, which ensnares both Toshio and his son Taichiro. <br /><br />Comparisons to Lolita are almost inevitable because of the man-child relationship, but the similarity ends there. The events take place decades after the relationship has ended and young Keiko and her passion for retribution take over. <br /><br />I've been meaning to read this novel and other Kawabata works for many years now, and I can't say whether it was the subject matter of a mature man having relations with a teenager that made me uncomfortable (of course it did), or the fact I listened to this instead of eye-reading it. The writing is apparently one of the attractions of this Nobel laureate's novels, but it was lost on me here, and I was only able to focus on the rather sordid story. It was good, but not a memorable experience. I'll definitely be reading his other books in print or ebook format to give myself a chance to enjoy the prose next time, albeit in translation. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13126879650570671003" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Kawabata's writing; a well written (and well translated) story, interesting, very emotional, engaging, a book hard to put down. Also a lot of traditional Japanese culture and little ceremonies so much different to western world. Still a lot of truth and reality in this book. <br/>"Beauty and sadness " was published in 1964, seems to me a fairly modern and open novel.<br/>Great!</span>

<div id="review_1916712914" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1916712914" />
    <a title="Tsung" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4992851-tsung"><img alt="Tsung" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298808605p2/4992851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1916712914?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tsung" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tsung" href="/user/show/4992851-tsung">Tsung</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1916712914" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5897158266341331516"><i>”Shall we play dolphin?”</i><br/><br/>I’m really, really torn about this one. I wanted to give up but the revenge plot kept me in. Excuse the schizoid review but that’s how it goes.<br/><br/>Once again Kawabata creates an enchanting world with vivid descriptions and luscious prose. If nothing else, Kyoto has got to be on my must visit list.<br/><br/>But then there is the sordid plot.<br/><br/><a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[Middle aged married guy, Oki, violates an underage girl, Otoko, gets her pregnant, miscarries, tries to kill herself, his wife Fumiko</span></span>
  <span id="freeText5897158266341331516" style="display:none"><i>”Shall we play dolphin?”</i><br /><br />I’m really, really torn about this one. I wanted to give up but the revenge plot kept me in. Excuse the schizoid review but that’s how it goes.<br /><br />Once again Kawabata creates an enchanting world with vivid descriptions and luscious prose. If nothing else, Kyoto has got to be on my must visit list.<br /><br />But then there is the sordid plot.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Middle aged married guy, Oki, violates an underage girl, Otoko, gets her pregnant, miscarries, tries to kill herself, his wife Fumiko also miscarries, he writes a book about the affair, Fumiko helps to type the book, he makes a lot of money, everybody accepts the situation and life goes on. What?!<br /><br />Time does not diminish the lingering feelings between Oki and Otoko, but Otoko also has a young pupil and lover Keiko. Keiko is seductive, smart, talented, possessive, jealous, manipulative, conniving and vindictive. She shags horny old Oki and almost gets cosy with his naïve son. She wants nothing more than revenge on Oki. While it spices up the story, especially with the cliff hanger, open ending, it is a little bit incongruous. Although she plays the villainess, Keiko is the most intriguing and interesting of all the characters. The others seem rather dull, isolated and passive by comparison. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />That out of the way, once again Kawabata exploits all the five senses in his unique way that transports you into the story setting. He splashes colours into the black and white pages.<br /><br /><b>Colours</b><br /><br />Fifty shades of green. How does one tell from a painting of a tea plantation that the painting is about a woman? Or that painting of a plum tree is about a woman?<br /><br />Fifty shades of pink. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[This one is about nipples so all I can say is, titillating.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>Sounds</b><br /><br /><i>As the bell tolled on he stopped straining to listen to it, and then he heard a sound that only a magnificent old bell could produce, a sound that seemed to roar forth with all the latent power of a distant world.</i><br /><br /><b>Smells</b><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The smell of a woman. Perspiration… <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />There are various references to the arts and history. But it is presence of certain themes which ultimately hold the story together.<br /><br /><b>Loneliness</b><br /><br /><i>That revolving chair in the observation car, turning by itself, came before. It was as if he saw his own loneliness silently turning round and round within his heart.</i><br /><br /><b>Memory</b><br /><br /><i>As the same memories kept recurring to his mind they became increasingly vivid. Events of over twenty years ago were more alive to him than those of yesterday.</i><br /><br /><b>Time</b><br /><br /><i>”Even philosophers don’t seem to have any satisfactory explanation of time. People say time will solve everything, but I have my doubts about that, too… Is death the end of it all?”</i><br /><br /><i>Time passed. But time flows in many streams. Like a river, an inner stream of time will flow rapidly at some places and sluggishly at others, or perhaps even stand hopelessly stagnant. Cosmic time is the same for everyone, but human time differs with each person. Time flows in the same way for all human beings; every human being flows through time in a different way.</i><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5897158266341331516" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

All of the characters in this look are manipulative to a certain degree, even our favorites.   <span id="freeText16844236058089686271" style="display:none">Beauty and Sadness tells of how people damage one another--through greed, seduction, and even through art. All of the characters in this look are manipulative to a certain degree, even our favorites. One of the characters was so blatently irrational that I couldn't tell if Kawabata meant for her to be a farse. <br /><br />It's the type of book that I appreciate more after I've read it and start thinking about it, rather than during. I know in the future certain scenes or quotes will pop into my mind. <br /><br />A defining characteristic of Kawabata is how he evokes feelings of isolation and sadness through his landscapes and descriptions of natural settings. Though he might go overboard at times with this, when it's done just right it can be extremely powerful. </span>

<div id="review_2882770494" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2882770494" />
    <a title="Mizuki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16842102-mizuki"><img alt="Mizuki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368277474p2/16842102.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2882770494?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mizuki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mizuki" href="/user/show/16842102-mizuki">Mizuki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2882770494" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7474188061678119055">2.5 stars.<br/><br/>The famed Japanese novelist Yasunari Kawabata wrote many novels which focused mostly on the beauty of Japanese traditional cultures, the scenery and the relationship between men and women, and his novels are often a 'hit or miss' for me.<br/><br/>After enjoying his elegantly written novels such as <i>The Old Capitol</i>, <i>Snow Country</i> and <i>Sleeping Beauties</i>; <i>Beauty and Sadness</i> really feels like a miss to me.<br/><br/>Once again, the backdrop is set in Koyto, though it's interesting to see how Mr. Kawabata describe</span>
  <span id="freeText7474188061678119055" style="display:none">2.5 stars.<br /><br />The famed Japanese novelist Yasunari Kawabata wrote many novels which focused mostly on the beauty of Japanese traditional cultures, the scenery and the relationship between men and women, and his novels are often a 'hit or miss' for me.<br /><br />After enjoying his elegantly written novels such as <i>The Old Capitol</i>, <i>Snow Country</i> and <i>Sleeping Beauties</i>; <i>Beauty and Sadness</i> really feels like a miss to me.<br /><br />Once again, the backdrop is set in Koyto, though it's interesting to see how Mr. Kawabata described the ancient city, still the male lead of the story is an asshole no matter how you look at it. I know fictional characters don't have to be role models or saints but I don't have anything to root for such a character at all. I know Mr. Kawabata's male characters are always a bit on the asshole-ish side but this time a male lead who had impregnated a 17 years old girl while he was already married by that time really is crossing the line. (Plus this male lead also happens to be a novelist, ha!)<br /><br />The male lead's wife and how she was jealous of her husband's younger mistress is mentioned in passing but hardly anything comes out of it.<br /><br />The only interesting part is the painter (who was once the male lead's young mistress years ago) and her pupil and the homosexual relationship between them. It's rumored that said relationship between the two women is inspired by Mr. Kawabata's relationship with a male friend. However, neither the painter nor the pupil have much in-depth insight to offer when art and female's sensuality is concerned. <br /><br />If you have a lot of patience, you can try this book out,I guess.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7474188061678119055" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3516636098" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3516636098" />
    <a title="Taruna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20403981-taruna"><img alt="Taruna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545486256p2/20403981.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3516636098?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Taruna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Taruna" href="/user/show/20403981-taruna">Taruna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141192611" href="/book/show/9248070-beauty-and-sadness">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3516636098" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11965772214063207512">What a beautiful book. It always amazes me how people are capable of writing about the most ambiguous of feelings so well. I myself am a person who often falls prey to nametags to understand people and myself, but it is books such as these that remind me just how complex human emotions and feelings are. Earlier this year, I had a similar experience reading Milan Kundera’s the unbearable lightness of being.<br/><br/>The writing is so elegant and poignant, its delicacy also reminded me of James Baldwin’s Gi</span>
  <span id="freeText11965772214063207512" style="display:none">What a beautiful book. It always amazes me how people are capable of writing about the most ambiguous of feelings so well. I myself am a person who often falls prey to nametags to understand people and myself, but it is books such as these that remind me just how complex human emotions and feelings are. Earlier this year, I had a similar experience reading Milan Kundera’s the unbearable lightness of being.<br /><br />The writing is so elegant and poignant, its delicacy also reminded me of James Baldwin’s Giovanni’s room. One can feel a sense of nostalgia throughout the book, as well as a deep admiration for Japanese history, art, culture and landscape. Beautiful descriptions of nature, loss and suffering. I especially loved Keiko’s character - bold, unapologetic but so fragile and insecure. <br /><br />I was very uncomfortable with the premise (30 year old man Oki gets a 15 year old girl Otoko pregnant while he has a wife) and a certain disgust with Oki remained with me throughout the book. Otoko’s supposed love for him that still remains after 2 decades can be seen in so many lights - trauma, longing, delusion, a sort of ignorance of having been violated. The writing again is so poignant and truthful that one does not judge her for loving the man, instead sees through her. <br /><br />There was something about the feeling of this book that seemed to spread a quiet around me. I wish I had finished it in one or two sittings. I would definitely recommend it to anyone interested in understanding complex emotions, Japanese aesthetics, nostalgic books. And also to sit through it in one go to really feel its immensity. <br /><br />Definitely looking forward to read more of Kawabata</span>
  <a data-text-id="11965772214063207512" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8100351503847496999">So. A friend told me, “don’t try to make too much sense from the book.” I tried to keep her wise advice in mind and that’s what kept me going through this strange book. <br/><br/>That and the fact that there were glorious descriptions of Kyoto, one of my favorite cities, bringing back warm memories of my visit there. But most of the characters behave in strange fashion, as the friend wrote, “there’s a touch of madness” in all of them. Oh well, aren’t we all?<br/><br/>I think the book makes more sense to me as an un</span>
  <span id="freeText8100351503847496999" style="display:none">So. A friend told me, “don’t try to make too much sense from the book.” I tried to keep her wise advice in mind and that’s what kept me going through this strange book. <br /><br />That and the fact that there were glorious descriptions of Kyoto, one of my favorite cities, bringing back warm memories of my visit there. But most of the characters behave in strange fashion, as the friend wrote, “there’s a touch of madness” in all of them. Oh well, aren’t we all?<br /><br />I think the book makes more sense to me as an understanding of the human psyche rather than an enjoyable book in itself. I can probably sit with a cup of tea and ruminate over each of the character’s actions. And that might be far more pleasing than the time I spent reading the book.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2770844901" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer576478343400595142">Alternative title: Never Marry a Writer<br/><br/>You shouldn't really. I make my money by my craft, and you shouldn't marry me. It's impossible not to incorporate bits of your life into your writing. And if, like the protagonist of Beauty and Sadness, you are a bit of a heel to begin with, it will be far worse. This is more of Kawabata facing down modern Japan and I'm guessing facing down his own relationship to his metier, and it's one of the better books of his that I've read.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_88792913" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/88792913" />
    <a title="Evan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1248986-evan"><img alt="Evan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488574589p2/1248986.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/88792913?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evan" href="/user/show/1248986-evan">Evan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780399505294" href="/book/show/1188245.Beauty_and_Sadness">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=asia">asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=2010-reads">2010-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=yearning">yearning</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=__in-my-collection">__in-my-collection</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=kawabata">kawabata</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=age-gap">age-gap</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer88792913" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16818610357791536211">My fourth Kawabata book, this and two others being novels and another a collection of short stories...<br/>I don't know if ruminations about the sea and stone gardens and cherry blossoms and fireflies or whatever make this story any less the lurid soap opera. Nonetheless, it's all a framework for what Kawabata does best, about which I elaborate below.<br/>The story, in a nutshell, is told partly in retrospect and partly in the present. A novelist in his 50s, Oki, recalls how at age 30 (when he was newly m</span>
  <span id="freeText16818610357791536211" style="display:none">My fourth Kawabata book, this and two others being novels and another a collection of short stories...<br />I don't know if ruminations about the sea and stone gardens and cherry blossoms and fireflies or whatever make this story any less the lurid soap opera. Nonetheless, it's all a framework for what Kawabata does best, about which I elaborate below.<br />The story, in a nutshell, is told partly in retrospect and partly in the present. A novelist in his 50s, Oki, recalls how at age 30 (when he was newly married) he had an affair with a 15-year-old girl, Otoko. She became pregnant, with tragic consequences, and they parted soon after. After a failed suicide, Otoko becomes a notable painter. Fast forward 24 years later, he has become a successful writer, largely on the profits of a novel very much based on their affair. There is fallout from this, including lingering jealousy from Oki's wife and new jealousy from Otoko's young teen artist protege, Keiko. Oki's son soon finds himself drawn into the intrigues. Keiko is masterminding a kind of replay of history -- but with a different result to set the score right. The plot contrivances from there I'll leave alone, other than to say that an elaborate web is woven in which past issues are dredged up and come to a head. Keiko is the least defined character, and yet she's the one who kicks the story into gear, while everything surrounding it is muted in typical Kawabata fashion; she seems like a deus ex machina more than anything. The full nature of Otoko's and Keiko's relationship, at least sexually, is never fully explored, which I found disappointing. Regardless of some of the plot absurdities (Kawabata wants to create a kind of circular arc that bridges past and present, but I'm not thorougly persuaded by how he does it) he is still a master of creating mood and a sense of yearning and exploring the memories, regrets and wisdom of mid-life and old age and reflecting on the follies and attractiveness of youth and the mysteries of time and love, and that's primarily why I like reading him.<br />His descriptions of Kyoto, Japan's old capital, are so evocative you want to catch the first plane to see it. As usual, there are erotic obsessions and motifs seen in the other Kawabata books; he seems to have a fetish or predilection for feet, fingers in the mouth and the notion of older guys shagging young girls. The book was a mixed bag for me; the ending seemed headed about where I suspected it would -- lets call it a karmic tit for tat, or something. Spoilers prevent me from saying much more. The narrative is layered and mixes memory and the present, deftly for the most part. The main characters in this are artists or scholars, so Kawabata has plenty of room in which to consider artistic concerns. He is especially good at evoking links between historical eras and the present, and also at considering the identities characters have with generations past, including the immediate ties with their parents, especially how we reflect our own parents genetically and spiritually. Things are slowly revealed and Kawabata's style as usual is langorous and dreamy. The story about the exhumed princess skeleton holding a glass plate photo of her lover which fades to blankness with exposure to the air is lovely; possibly the best metaphor in the book about the fleetingness of time and love, and the secrets that lovers carry to their graves.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16818610357791536211" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('s3SJvYpwntnaYJqX+PUlUc8odkCLajx5pTKEBRMEpXhdjk70ZbnIs7K6WqSJMcUDGI3jI+WZseI0kDNv5UcxJA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Murakami\'s image-intensive narrative paints a portrait of a group of friends locked in a destructive cycle of sex, drugs and rock\'n\'roll. The novel is all but plotless, but the raw and often violent prose takes us on a rollercoaster ride through reality and hallucination, highs and lows, in which the characters and their experiences come vividly to life. Trapped in passivity, they gain neither passion nor pleasure from their adventures. Yet out of the alienation, boredom and underlying rage and grief emerges a strangely quiet and almost equally shocking beauty. Ryu Murakami\'s first novel, Almost Transparent Blue won the coveted Akutagawa literary prize and became an instant bestseller. Representing a sharp and conscious turning away from the introspective trend of postwar Japanese literature, it polarized critics and public alike and soon attracted international attention as an Among Japanese readers both young and old it has enjoyed a timeless popularity, making it, according to Donald Keene, &quot;probably the most widely read novel in modern Japan.&quot;\n\nThe setting is Japan\'s deep south, where the author himself spent some time teaching English in a boys\' school. Into this conservative world, with its social proprieties and established pecking order, breezes Botchan, down from the big city, with scant respect for either his elders or his noisy young charges; and the result is a chain of collisions large and small.\n\nMuch of the story seems to occur in summer, against the drone of cicadas, and in many ways this is a summer book light, funny, never slow-moving. Here, in a lively new translation much better suited to Western tastes than any of its forebears, Botchan\'s homespun appeal is all the more apparent, and even those who have never been near the While an acolyte at the temple, he fixates on the structure’s aesthetic perfection and it becomes the one and only object of his desire. But as Mizoguchi begins to perceive flaws in the temple, he determines that the only true path to beauty lies in an act of horrendous violence. Based on a real incident that occurred in 1950, The Temple of the Golden Pavilion brilliantly portrays the passions and agonies of a young man in postwar Japan, bringing to the subject the erotic imagination and instinct for the dramatic moment that marked Mishima as one of the towering makers of modern fiction. When one night they are forced to expel a middle-aged man and a prostitute from their room, Mari finds herself drawn to the man\'s voice, in what will become the first gesture of a single long seduction. In spite of her provincial surroundings, and her cool but controlling mother, Mari is a sophisticated observer of human desire, and she sees in this man something she has long been looking for.\n\nThe man is a proud if threadbare translator living on an island off the coast. A widower, there are whispers around town that he may have murdered his wife. Mari begins to visit him on his island, and he soon initiates her into a dark realm of both pain and pleasure, a place in which she finds herself more at ease even than the translator. La situación en Chile era muy difícil para los trabajadore<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11976535904573107242\" style=\"display:none\">El libro describe la trágica situación en la que vivían y morían los mineros chilenos a finales del siglo XIX y principios del XX (principalmente basándose en los mineros del carbón de Lota). La situación en Chile era muy difícil para los trabajadores, especialmente para los mineros, que trabajaban desde el amanecer hasta el anochecer en condiciones inhumanas.\n\nEn este contexto de desesperación, rabia e impotencia ante una situación social y económica que hacía sufrir aún más a los más desfavorecidos, el libro de Baldomero Lillo, es básicamente una novela descriptiva sobre la vida en la mina, y la vida de sus mineros; asimismo es una crítica en contra del poder explotador, que reducía la condición humana de los mineros a simples bestias: «¡Pobre viejo, te echan porque ya no sirves! Lo mismo nos pasa a todos. Allí abajo no se hace distinción entre el hombre y la bestia». <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7537136835550288756">Yasunari <b>Kawabata</b> (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/846386._' rel='nofollow noopener'>川端 康成</a>) was a Japanese short story writer and novelist whose spare, lyrical, subtly-shaded prose works won him the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1968, the first Japanese author to receive the award. His works have enjoyed broad international appeal and are still widely read today.<br/><br/>Nobel Lecture: 1968<br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prizes/literature/laureates/1968/kawabata-lecture.html'>http://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prize...</a></span>
  <span id="freeText7537136835550288756" style="display:none">Yasunari <b>Kawabata</b> (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/846386._" rel="nofollow noopener">川端 康成</a>) was a Japanese short story writer and novelist whose spare, lyrical, subtly-shaded prose works won him the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1968, the first Japanese author to receive the award. His works have enjoyed broad international appeal and are still widely read today.<br /><br />Nobel Lecture: 1968<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prizes/literature/laureates/1968/kawabata-lecture.html">http://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prize...</a></span>
